“Why didn’t you see him?”

Agatha’s eyebrows furrow, confused.

“You’ve been on the run for thirty years,” Rio says. “You didn’t realize this guy was tailing you? You didn’t see him sneaking around your car?”

“Oh.” Agatha scoffs. “Okay. We’re blaming the victim now?”

Rio doesn’t answer, and a heavy silence descends between them. Rio can see Agatha’s face transform as she realizes what Rio’s really asking; she watches as something seems to shutter behind her eyes, close off. Watches her curl her lip disdainfully.

“I see,” she says finally. “You think I set you up. And how am I supposed to have done that, exactly? I didn’t even know you were here.”

“No?”

“No.”

“But this is what you wanted, right?” Rio spits, and suddenly all of her fear and adrenaline has a point, a perfect target. “This is exactly what you wanted. You wanted me to – you’ve been trying to make me—”

“Make you what?” Agatha interrupts. “I didn’t make you do anything. I was sitting in my car.” Her mouth curves into a cold, furious smile. “Don’t blame me just because it felt good.”

Every muscle in Rio’s body is trembling, coiled up, ready to explode. “It did not feel good,” she growls.

It didn’t. It felt – overwhelming, powerful, terrifying, but it didn’t feel—

“Holding a man’s life in your hands?” Agatha's eyes are shrewd, her voice knowing. “Deciding whether he lives or dies? Don’t tell me that didn’t feel good.”

“It didn’t—”

“Stopping him from taking what’s yours? That felt good.”

“It did not feel good!” Rio shouts, and Agatha gives her a slow smile.

“Ooh,” she says in a mocking voice. “The lady doth protest. Admit it, sweetheart. You liked it. You loved it.”

“No, you love it. You’re the one who kills people for fun. Not me.”

“Oh, come on. You didn’t enjoy it at all?” Agatha asks in a sly voice. “Not even a little?”

“See,” Rio says in a shaking voice. “This is what I mean – you want me to be as twisted as you are.”

“Oh, please,” Agatha laughs mirthlessly. “This is about what you want, sweet pea.”

"You want me to like hurting people so you won't have to feel alone.” Rio spits the words like an accusation, hoping they’ll sting, hoping they’ll wound, but Agatha’s smug expression doesn’t change. She’s looking at Rio like she’s seen something true about her, like she knows her better than Rio knows herself.

“You beat the shit out of him,” she says.

“To protect you!”

“You could have arrested him. But you didn’t, did you?” Agatha spreads her hands. “So what does that tell you?”

Rio clenches her teeth so hard her jaw aches. All her retorts die in her throat. Agatha’s right: she followed, she attacked. She brought the tire iron down again and again, and she hadn’t been thinking about justice, or the law, or doing the right thing.

She’d been thinking: mine.

“I am nothing like you.” Rio can hear how desperate she sounds, her protests ringing hollow even to her own ears. She can argue all she wants: there’s a man bleeding to death at her feet.

“Okay, honey,” Agatha says dryly. “Whatever you need to tell yourself.”

Bile burns its way up Rio’s throat. She wants to undo this; she wants this not to have happened. She wants to wipe that condescending fucking smile off Agatha’s face and push her away, back into the nightmarish darkness where she belongs.

“I am nothing like you,” she snaps. “You are a monster.”

Agatha’s smile doesn’t waver, but her eyes narrow almost imperceptibly. “Am I?”

Not enough. Rio needs her to rise to the bait. She needs to hate her.

“Why would I run away with you?” she asks viciously, taking a step forward. “You’re alone. You have nothing and no one. What kind of life could you possibly offer me?”

There – Agatha’s smile falters, just for a second. But Rio sees it, seizes on it.

“Poor sad serial killer who no one ever loved,” she taunts, the words dripping venom. “You want to know why I could never be like you? Because I’m not a fucking psychopath. Because I’m not broken. That’s what you are – you are broken, Agatha. You are damaged beyond fucking repair.” Her voice is threaded through with something like rage, something like self-loathing. “No wonder your own mother couldn’t love you.”

Rio’s heartbeat thuds in her ears as she waits for Agatha’s growled retort, the inevitable retaliation, the violent hand wrapping around her throat. She’s poised and ready to fight.

But all Agatha does, in the end, is lift her chin.

“Fine.” She sniffs, lofty and disdainful, as though all of this is beneath her. A tear slides down her cheek, but she ignores it. “Go back to your pathetic little life, then. See how that works out for you.”

She holds Rio’s gaze, the implied threat hanging between them, but Rio knows better than to back down now. She can’t flinch, can’t waver.

“I’m calling this in,” she says, giving her a cold smile. “Better start packing.”

Agatha’s mouth curls, eyes still glittering. She looks like she could kill Rio where she stands. But instead she shakes the hair off her face, turns on her heel, and walks into the house.

It takes a full trembling, shuddering minute before Rio remembers to tell her body to move, and another long moment before it responds. She crouches down and picks up the tire iron. She forces herself to look over at the man. His chest is still rising and falling, shallow but steady.

She walks into the grass on unsteady legs, searching for her phone where Agatha threw it.

“Come on,” she murmurs, sweeping her feet over the grass until her toe finally meets something hard in the darkness. She grabs it and walks to her car, climbing inside and locking the doors.

She speaks quickly when the 911 operator answers.

“There’s been an assault,” she hears herself say, managing to keep her voice steady despite the chattering of her teeth. “Send an ambulance to—” She gives the address, and hangs up before they can ask questions or trace the call.

She stares at the house through her windshield. A light comes on upstairs, then another. She can see Agatha's silhouette moving quickly, urgently.

She’ll have a go-bag ready, Rio knows. Probably has another car nearby. She’s been doing this a long time.

Sirens wail in the distance, getting closer, and the upstairs lights go dark. Agatha doesn’t come out the front door. She must have fled out the back. Good.

Rio starts her car.

She drives the wrong way for a while, north and west, toward the Potomac. Away from Agatha’s house. Away from Agatha. She pulls into an empty parking lot on the water’s edge. It takes ten or fifteen minutes for her to find the strength to leave the car.

Wind rustles through trees overhead; the river flows swiftly at her feet. The tire iron makes a dull splash before it sinks to the bottom.

Rio watches the ripples spread until they disappear.

 

x

 

She wakes to the sound of sirens. Her eyes fly open, her dream bleeding into reality: terror, the desperate need to escape. She sits up, heart pounding.

Alarm, she realizes finally. She fumbles with her phone, punching at the screen. It’s just the fucking—

The wailing stops. She collapses back into bed, breathing hard, staring up at the ceiling. Her eyes are scratchy and dry, her body aching like she's been in a fight. Her hands—

She holds them up, examining them. She'd come home a few hours ago and showered, scrubbing every part of herself with scalding water: under her fingernails, her scalp, between her toes. Then she'd stripped the sheets that still smelled like Agatha and crawled beneath the duvet. She hadn’t even bothered drying off.

In the dim light of morning, her hands look raw and tender, but clean. No blood under her nails, no lingering evidence.

Except the man himself.

Or his body.

She has no way of knowing if he’s alive or dead. The ambulance came, she’d made sure of it, but that doesn't mean anything. Critical injuries, skull fractures – people die from less. And they survive worse. There's no way to know.

She sits up, pressing her palms to her eyes. This is pointless. She'll know soon enough if she's a murderer.

She releases her eyes and reaches for her phone on the nightstand. No messages since last night, and no missed calls, not that she’d expected anything different. Agatha’s gone, vanished back into whatever dark corner of the universe she’d emerged from.

It doesn’t matter. Rio doesn’t care.

“I don’t care,” she says aloud, testing the feel of the words in her mouth. She almost sounds like she believes it.

 

There’s a part of her that expects to be accosted at the door, arrested the moment she enters the FBI building. But when she arrives it’s just like any other day – no alarms sound when she scans her badge, no one stops her on the way to the elevator. No one’s waiting for her in her office. All the files she’d left on her desk yesterday are still sitting where she left them, untouched.

She spends her morning alternating between staring blankly down at her work and staring blankly out the window. It’s not that she’s unaccustomed to violence – she’s never killed anyone (yet, she thinks, maybe) but she’s been an agent for a long time. She’s seen her share of dead bodies, witnessed the terrible things that people can do to one another. She’s had tough arrests. She’s put people in the hospital. This isn’t her first time drawing blood.

But she’d always been able to reassure herself that she’d done those things because it was her job. She isn’t immune to all the criticisms of law enforcement – she has her reservations about representing the long arm of the law, and she knows how flimsy an excuse ‘I was just following orders’ is. But she’s always figured that someone has to do it. It’s the way society has decided to maintain order; it’s the way things work. Better that it’s her out there – measured, calm, reasonable – than some asshole who just wants to feel powerful because he gets to hold a gun.

Except she wasn’t anything like measured or calm or reasonable last night. She didn’t identify herself as an agent. She didn’t give the man a chance to back away. She just attacked. And she didn’t stop, even after he was down. Even after she felt the sickening crunch of bone—

“Hey, you coming or what?”

Rio’s head snaps up. Jen is standing in her doorway, eyebrows raised.

“Department meeting?” Jen supplies when Rio just stares at her, wide-eyed and mute.

“Yeah,” Rio rasps. She clears her throat. “Sorry, yeah. Lost track of time. Let me just—”

She glances around her desk, grabbing a pen and a legal pad. Something to look at during the meeting. A reason to keep her head down.

Lilia’s already addressing the room from a podium in front when Rio and Jen arrive. Rio slips into her usual chair next to Alice.

“Are you sick?” Alice whispers, leaning toward her. “You look like shit.”

“Thanks,” Rio whispers back.

“McCreedy and Prieto, I want you two on this one,” Lilia is saying. “Coordinate with the IRS, for all the help they’re likely to be, and let me know if you run into any issues arranging surveillance. Alright, let’s see… Vidal. Updates on the Harkness case?”

Rio’s mouth goes dry. “Yeah. We, uh – well, we were able to trace the anesthetic she used at the gala. Alice, do you want to—?”

Rio regrets the words as soon as they leave her mouth. Alice hasn’t wanted to talk about that night with anyone; of course she’s not going to want to give an update to the entire department about the drug that took them both out. Rio had tried to check in with her, once, a few days after they were released from the hospital – some fumbling attempt at Hey, are you okay? – but Alice had shrugged and said, Let's just find her. And she’d meant it. She’s been working late, coming in early. The messy, scattered Alice seems to be gone, replaced by someone grim, someone with a steely-eyed focus. Some days Rio feels like she hardly recognizes her.

But she can see her for what she is: one more person she’s damaged with all her lies. One more consequence she can’t undo.

“It was a pharmaceutical-grade anesthetic,” Alice says, her tone clipped, carefully even. “Sevoflurane in aerosol form. The concentration and delivery method suggest someone with a knowledge of chemistry and access to medical supplies, but that’s not surprising given Harkness’s MO with cyanide.”

“We’re still working on tracing the source,” Rio adds. “But it’s tough to narrow it down since it’s widely used. She could have stolen it from a hospital, a vet’s office, a research facility – there’s a lot of options.”

“Okay, got it. Any other leads? Where are we on tracking her whereabouts?”

“Well, there’ve been no confirmed sightings,” Rio says, throat tight. “The credit card she used to buy the ticket to the gala was stolen, like we figured – it would have been easy enough for her to buy a number off the dark web, or just steal a card herself. As for the remaining targets, we’re monitoring them as best we can. Dunn and Kumar have opted to stick with their private security, even after what happened with Sanders. And Mills will only speak to us through a lawyer. He swears he has no idea where his client lives.”

“Hm,” Lilia says skeptically. “Well – keep at it. She’ll go after one of those three eventually, and we want to be ready to take her when she does.” She flips through a notepad on the podium in front of her. “Okay, next item, we caught a new case overnight. Unidentified male, somebody beat the crap out of him. He’s still a John Doe, but his prints were flagged in the system as a person of interest in cases over a few states. Given the circumstances in those cases, there’s some suspicion we might be looking at a contract killer.”

Rio’s entire body flushes hot, breath catching as her chest seizes. She clutches her hands into fists in her lap and tries to control her expression.

Lilia snaps the file closed and walks it over to Jen, dropping it in front of her. “Kale, you’re up. Go see what we’ve got. And Vidal, I want you to go with her. We might be looking at a missing persons case here, too – they can’t find the person who’d been renting the house where he was assaulted. Whole thing’s a mess. But start at the hospital, see if he’s in any position to talk, and we’ll go from there.” She pulls off her reading glasses and surveys the room. “That’s all, folks. Go be excellent. Dismissed.”

The meeting breaks up, people gathering files and coffee mugs. Rio trails behind Jen as they head toward the elevator, her mind stuttering over the same thought on repeat: He's alive. I didn’t kill him. He’s alive. He lived. He’s alive.

But her relief is threaded through with a sickly, rancid nausea. The thing is, she's not relieved because she values human life, because she’s grateful this man managed to survive – she's relieved because she's not a murderer. Because a dead body would have complicated everything.

Except a living witness might be worse.

What if he heard her voice? Saw her face? What if he wakes up and tells them there was someone else there, a woman, and suddenly they're looking for a second person at the scene and Rio has to investigate her own crime while pretending she has no idea who could have been involved—

She's relieved he's alive. She's terrified he's alive. All of it grates inside her like broken glass.

“Hello,” Jen says, snapping her fingers in front of Rio’s face. "You're doing that thing again."

"What thing?"

"That thousand-yard stare thing." Jen hits the elevator button. “I swear, you walk around looking like a haunted Victorian child half the time.”

Rio’s too exhausted to muster up a snarky retort. “I’m just thinking about the case."

"Which one? Harkness or the new guy?"

Same thing, she thinks. There’s just the one.

“New guy,” she says.

“Yeah. Contract killer should be fun. I wonder who did it,” Jen says as the elevator arrives. “I mean, you don't just stumble into beating the shit out of a professional hitman.”

Rio steps into the elevator, the walls suddenly feeling very close.

“No,” she says. “I guess you don’t.”

 

x

 

A detective is waiting to brief them at the hospital, a sharp-eyed woman, no-nonsense, already gesturing them over. Rio hangs back, lets Jen take the lead. It’s her case. And anyway, the last time Rio let herself get close to a local detective, she ended up – well, here. Standing outside a hospital room, about to face a man whose skull she cracked open.

She heads toward the uniform posted at the door instead. Older guy, probably close to retirement. Hopefully less likely to clock it if Rio seems a little… off.

“How’s he doing?” she asks, gesturing toward the room with her chin.

The officer shrugs. “Still out. Seems like it could go either way, from what the docs are saying.”

Rio nods in a way that she hopes seems thoughtful, composed. And then she makes herself step inside the room.

She hadn’t gotten a good look at the man’s face in the darkness; he’d been a slinking shadow before she hit him, and she’d done her best not to look at him after. But here is the undeniable proof of what she’d done, rendered in fluorescent hospital lighting: the swelling, the bruising spreading down the side of his face like spilled ink, the ventilator tube. Rough skin, a bandage wrapped around his head, shaggy brown hair poking out below. Dark circles around both eyes, like a raccoon. There’s a steady beeping sound coming from the heart monitor next to the bed. His eyes don’t move beneath his eyelids.

“Well, this is a no-go.” Rio jumps, startled, as Jen appears beside her at the man’s bedside. “Local PD said there’s been no change since he got here. He’s not going to be giving us a statement anytime soon.”

Rio clears her throat. “What’s the prognosis?” she asks, eyes still glued to the man.

“Not great. He’s got a depressed skull fracture and brain swelling. They removed part of his skull to relieve the pressure, but it’s touch and go.”

They removed part of his skull. Rio’s stomach clenches.

“Did they say anything about the attack?” She tries to wet her lips, to sound less like she’s croaking the words out. “Or the weapon?”

“Yeah,” Jen says, glancing down at her notepad. “Three distinct impacts. Two on the left parietal region, one on the left temporal. Weapon was something cylindrical, a pipe or a crowbar or something. First hit took him down from behind, and then the second and third drove skull fragments into the brain tissue.”

The ferrous scent of blood in the air; the pathetic sound he’d made as he fell. The crack and crunch of bone. Rio’s own growl low in her chest: Stay down.

Jen doesn’t seem to notice her shudder; she’s checking her phone, scrolling through her texts. “Oh, nice. Alice has something on the house. It was a rental – some business was using it for employees who came through. She’s working on getting the name of the person who’d been staying there.”

“Maybe it was empty,” Rio says, voice tremulous. “Maybe that’s why we can’t find anyone.”

“Guess we’ll see.” Jen lifts her head. “Ready?”

“Yeah,” Rio says. “I’m just, uh – I’ve gotta run to the bathroom.”

Jen nods distractedly, head down again, texting Alice back. “‘Kay. Meet you at the elevator.”

Rio pushes her way into the bathroom and stands in front of the sink, gazing dully at her reflection. Alice was right: she does look like shit. Pallid skin, red-rimmed eyes.

She just needs to get through this day. Go to the scene, examine the blood-stained driveway like she’s never seen it before. Walk through the house and pretend she doesn’t know whose footsteps she’s stepping into, whose air she’s breathing. Local PD will be canvassing, looking for anyone who might have heard anything, or who might have gotten camera footage. But Rio knows they’re not going to find anything. Agatha doesn’t want to get caught this time, so she won’t. The houses in that neighborhood are spaced out – little manors with large, luxurious yards, massive hedges blocking sightlines and muffling sounds. And there was no one directly across the street from Agatha’s house. There isn’t going to be any camera footage. Agatha would have factored that in, made sure of it before she rented the place.

Rio closes her eyes. You can do this. Just make it to the end of the day. You’ve just got to make it to the end.

She pushes herself off the sink and opens the bathroom door.

There’s a flurry of activity down the hall. Jen strides quickly past her, away from the elevators and back toward the hitman’s room.

“It’s our guy,” she calls to Rio without slowing down.

A shout of “Clear!” rings down the hallway, followed by the sound of a high-pitched whine, a pulsing thud. “Resume compressions and give me 10ccs of epi.”

Rio follows on trembling legs. She joins Jen to stand next to the uniform and the detective. They all stare into the room where the doctors and nurses are working.

No. This man cannot die.

“Clear!” Thud. The heart monitor shrieks a long, wailing tone. “I need another round of epi.”

No, no, no. Rio cannot have murdered this man.

“What happened?” she asks hoarsely.

Jen looks at her like she’s an idiot. “Uh, my guess would be the catastrophic brain injury.”

“Okay, pause compressions. Nope, let’s go again. Clear!”

His body jerks. The monitor continues its flat wail.

"One more time. Clear!"

His body contracts on the hospital bed, landing with the thud of the tire iron against his skull, the thud of his head bouncing against the pavement, the thud of a weapon landing at the bottom of a river.

“No, no, no, no, no,” Rio whispers. “Come on, come on, come on.”

“Pause compressions.” The high-pitched whine of the heart monitor doesn’t change, and the doctor sighs. “Okay.”

Rio’s fingernails are digging into her palms. “Wait,” she says in a tight voice.

But the nurses are already moving away from him, pulling off gloves, lowering masks. Someone turns off the heart monitor, the flurry of activity abruptly falling into silence.

“Time of death,” the doctor announces, glancing at the clock on the wall. But Rio doesn’t hear anything after the word death.

Oh, fuck.

Oh, fuck.

Rio blinks. She can’t tell if she sways, or if the roiling and pitching of the room is only in her mind.

I killed him.

I killed him.

“Officers?” a voice comes from inside the room. “You uh – you might want to see this.”

Rio follows Jen and the others into the room on numb feet.

“We opened his gown to administer the shocks,” a nurse says uneasily. “But, uh—” The nurse covers him again, arranging the fabric as it was before they started working on him.

The hitman lies motionless on the bed. And there, scrawled across his hospital gown in dark red lipstick, are three letters:

XO

- A

Chapter 10

“Cyanide,” Alice says flatly, dropping the medical examiner’s report onto Rio’s desk.

“We knew it would be.” Rio stares down at the report without touching it. She already knows what it’ll say: ferrocyanide salts, lethal concentration. Agatha’s familiar handiwork.

“We got the security footage?” Jen asks from where she’s perched on the edge of Rio’s desk.

“For all the good it does us.” Alice pulls up the video on her laptop, angling it so they can all see. A figure in scrubs strides down the hallway, purposeful, confident, hair hidden under a scrub cap, face masked and angled away from every camera. “She walked past hospital security, past nurses, doctors, the cop standing guard at the door, and she killed him.”

Rio watches the grainy footage loop: the figure entering the room, emerging less than a minute later, walking back down the hall. Completely calm. They’d shut down the hospital immediately, blocked all the exits, but it was too late. In the time it had taken the hitman to die, Agatha had vanished.

“What do we have on the house?” Rio asks.

“Shell corporation,” Jen says, looking at her notes. “Rented three months ago through an LLC that doesn’t exist anymore. Landlord thought it was housing rotating execs passing through DC. He never actually met the tenants.” She snaps her notebook closed. “We’re processing it now, but it’s not like she’s stupid enough to leave a forwarding address.”

“No,” Rio agrees. “She’s a lot of things, but stupid isn’t one of them.”

She glances at her phone, lying face-down on her desk. She’d tried texting yesterday – what the fuck is wrong with you????? why the FUCK would you do that??????????? – but the message hadn’t gone through. Either Agatha had ditched her burner, or she’d blocked Rio. Neither would surprise her.

“So that’s it,” Alice says. “We had a witness, and now he’s dead. We’ve got nothing.” She pushes herself up from her chair and starts pacing around the room. “What are we even doing here? This woman is running circles around us. We’re just – we’re just sitting here while she keeps killing people, and we’re no closer to catching her now than we were months ago. We need a plan, Rio. Give me a plan. Give me something to do besides watch her win.”

Rio forces herself to look at Alice, to meet her eye. “She’s not going to win.”

Amazing how she can still manage to say something like that with a straight face. But it feels true this time. She’s going to find Agatha, and she’s going to stop this.

“That man could have lived,” Alice says, gripping the back of her chair. “He could have lived. We were supposed to protect him.”

It’s the same refrain that keeps running through Rio’s own mind: the man could have lived. He should have lived. Rio had told Agatha she didn’t want her to kill him, had said and done whatever she had to do to keep her from killing him, and Agatha had just… done it anyway. Dragged Rio into the darkness, whether she wanted it or not.

As soon as Rio had seen those letters on his chest, all of her powerlessness, all of her vulnerability – all of it had crystallized into a white hot rage. Of course. Of course Agatha couldn’t even do this one fucking thing for her. And she’d known how much it mattered to Rio. It mattered so much Rio had given up Agatha in exchange for it. But even that hadn’t been enough to stop her. Agatha hadn’t cared.

But. She had signed her name.

Rio still hasn’t been able to reconcile that. Agatha didn’t have to take credit. The man’s death would have looked like the natural result of his injuries – there would have been no reason to check for poison, not after what Rio had already done to him. Even Rio had thought it was a heart attack. But Agatha had taken responsibility for it. She made sure that Rio wasn’t the reason he died, and she’d made sure Rio knew it. Signing her name to this murder made it look like Agatha had been the one who attacked him in the first place.

His murder felt like a slap in the face, because it was. But it was also a caress. It was a love letter and a fuck you, all at once.

She’d called Agatha broken. She’d used her mother as a weapon: no wonder she couldn’t love you. Agatha had asked her to run away together, and Rio had responded by reaching in and grabbing for the softest, most vulnerable parts of Agatha, ripping and shredding and tearing with teeth until there was nothing gentle left. Nothing that Rio could touch without bleeding.

Except there was something gentle left, somehow. Even now. Even in this. Agatha had killed the man, yes – but she'd protected Rio too. She’d made her complicit, but only the two of them knew it. She’d finished what Rio had started. Claimed her violence, and made it theirs.

It was sick. It was romantic. It was both.

With Agatha, it’s always both.

The room falls silent except for the sound of Alice’s footsteps slowly circling them. Jen is still staring at the laptop screen.

“Huh,” Jen says. Rio follows her eyes as she leans in, studying the looping footage.

“What?”

“Look. There. She’s holding the vial and syringe in her left hand.”

“So?” Alice asks.

Rio’s heart skips a beat; she knows what Jen is going to say before she says it.

“So whoever bashed our guy’s skull in was right-handed.”

Rio licks her lips with a dry tongue. “Holding something in her left hand doesn’t mean she’s a lefty.”

“Doesn’t mean she isn’t,” Jen counters.

“Okay, but why would she sneak into the hospital to poison someone she hadn’t tried to kill?” Alice asks.

“She wouldn’t,” Rio says. “She’s worked alone in every other case. There’s no reason she’d add an accomplice now.”

“You don’t know that,” Jen shoots at her. “You don’t know what she’d do.”

“Look, even if she is left-handed – we’re all frustrated,” Rio says. “I get it. But we can’t start chasing wild theories just because we’ve got—”

“Nothing?” Alice asks pointedly.

She’s still pacing around the room, and Jen reaches out and grabs her hand as she passes by. They exchange a look, and—

Oh.

It’s just a flicker, but Rio doesn’t miss it – she knows what it looks like to want to kiss someone at exactly the wrong time. When had that happened? Since Rio and Alice had been hospitalized, maybe? Or before? Rio remembers the way that Jen had looked at Alice that night, the way she’d comforted her. We need you, okay?

Jen’s gaze is steady, and Alice nods at her, taking a long, deep breath.

Well. Good for them. It’s good. It’s good that they have each other. And meanwhile, Rio has…

She has exactly what her choices have left her with.

She throws her pen down into her notebook, clenching and unclenching her fists. The room suddenly feels too small, the weight on her chest too heavy.

“It’s late,” she announces, breaking up the little spell between Alice and Jen. “We’ll come back to this tomorrow.”

 

x

 

She parks in front of Wanda’s house and lets her head fall back against the headrest.

She should have canceled. She has no right to be here, no right to walk into Wanda’s house and act like she’s a normal person, like she’s someone who didn’t help murder a man earlier this week. Like she hasn’t been putting Wanda in danger for months. But giving Wanda up… she just can’t. She can’t bring herself to do it. It’s not even about stopping Agatha from “winning” anymore. It’s about Rio maintaining one tether, one single fragile string that connects her to light and ease and an ordinary, unremarkable life. Wanda likes getting dressed up and going to dinner and a movie. She’s excited at the prospect of meeting Rio’s parents someday. She doesn’t even kill spiders.

She’s good. And Rio really, really needs good right now.

So she’s going to do this. She’s going to walk inside, kiss her girlfriend, and let everything else recede into the background for a little while. Pretend she’s good too.

“Hey, babe,” she calls as she walks in. She shucks off her coat and hangs it on the hook by the door. “Wow, that smells amazing.”

“Thanks.”

Chapter 11

Rio stands up at the sound of Wanda’s car pulling into the driveway. She’d spent the afternoon preparing – grocery shopping, picking her outfit, cleaning up around the house. At the last minute she’d changed the sheets on her bed. Not that she expects Wanda to spend the night. But… just in case.

The knock on her door is soft and tentative, which is different. Wanda usually just lets herself in.

“Hey,” Rio says, opening the door.

“Hi.” Wanda gives her a strained smile, and Rio can see her doing the same thing Rio’s doing – trying to be normal, wearing it like a costume, testing the weight of it.

It had taken three days for Wanda to respond to Rio’s texts, and Rio had spent all three of those days spiraling through one dark possibility after another: Wanda was done with her. She was meeting up with Agatha, talking in some cozy bar, heads together. She’d been kidnapped. She was tied up somewhere, terrified. She was dead.

When her phone finally lit up, Rio had answered before the first ring had finished.

“Hi,” she’d said quickly. “How are you? Are you okay?”

“Hi,” Wanda had responded, and at the sound of her voice Rio had taken her first full breath in days. “Yeah. I’m fine.”

“Good. Uh – good. I’m happy you called.”

“I wasn’t sure I should.”

Wanda’s voice had been formal, measured. She was talking to Rio like she was a stranger, but at least she was talking to her.

“Well, I’m glad you did.”

There was a long silence between them that Rio had no idea how to fill.

“So,” Wanda said finally. “I talked to Agnes.”

Rio’s stomach had dropped. Of course. Of course Agatha had gotten to her, poured god-knows-what poison into her ear. Rio pushed away the ember of anger that flared up inside her at the thought that Wanda had been willing to take Agatha’s call when she hadn’t bothered responding to Rio in days.

“Oh, yeah?”

“Yeah,” Wanda said. “She apologized. She said she had too much to drink at dinner and – I don’t know, it sounded like she thought we were interested in her or something? Like maybe she thought the three of us were going to… anyway. She seemed really embarrassed.”

“Oh,” Rio said dumbly.

“But she said you didn’t do anything wrong. She feels terrible about it.”

“Look, Wanda—”

“And I feel terrible about it, too,” Wanda had interrupted, the words seeming to tumble out of her. “I overreacted, and you’re probably angry with me, and – I get it. You told me it was nothing and I should have believed you. I just got so freaked out, and I made this whole story up in my head and – I’m really sorry, Rio.”

Listening to Wanda apologize to her had made a spot ache deep in Rio’s chest, somewhere behind her breastbone.

“You don’t have anything to be sorry for,” she’d said.

“Yes, I do. I was the one who—”

“How about this,” Rio had cut her off. “Let’s just forget it. What if we just forget about it and move on?”

“Really?” Wanda had asked. “Are you sure?”

“Yes. Please. I would really love to just pretend that whole night never happened.”

There was a long, uncertain silence. Rio knew Wanda had called to have some big talk, to air grievances and process feelings, but Rio just couldn’t. She wanted to be done with this. She needed this part to be over so she could get back to the work of moving on, leaving Agatha behind and becoming someone who never would have fallen for her in the first place.

Except she knew it wasn’t over, not really. This thing with Agatha – the apology, the contrition she offered instead of a bomb that would blow up Rio's relationship entirely – it was just more of the same. Another way of keeping her claws embedded in Rio's life. This was exactly what Agatha wanted, wasn’t it? Rio and Wanda, stitched back together with Agatha's own thread, so she could keep pulling at the seams.

So here they are. Moving forward inside the cage Agatha has built for them.

Wanda steps inside, slipping off her jacket. She's wearing jeans and a soft green sweater that brings out her eyes, and she looks so lovely and so fragile that Rio almost has to look away.

"Here, let me—” Rio reaches for the jacket at the same moment Wanda moves toward the coat hooks, and they do an awkward little shuffle, both of them pulling back, both of them smiling apologetically.

"Sorry," Rio says. This used to feel easy. Last week, this felt easy.

"No, I—" Wanda hangs up her own jacket. "Sorry."

They stand in the entryway. The distance between them – three feet, maybe – feels like a physical thing, like the ghost of another person. Wanda tucks a strand of hair behind her ear and looks around the room, taking inventory of it like she hasn't been here dozens of times.

"You moved the monstera,” she says.

“Oh. Yeah. He was getting too much sun.”

Wanda nods, still not quite looking at her. Her eyes land on the bookshelf, the window, the framed print above the radiator that she'd helped Rio hang a few weeks ago. The two of them had bickered cheerfully about whether it would look better there or in the bedroom. Wanda won. And she’d been right. It’s perfect there.

“So… wine?" Rio asks.

"Yes, please,” Wanda says.

Rio retreats to the kitchen, taking out two wine glasses and uncorking a wine bottle. She's hyper-aware of every sound – the glug of wine into glasses, the rustle of Wanda settling onto the couch, the distant hum of traffic outside. She puts a packet of popcorn into the microwave.

What am I doing? she thinks for the thousandth time. She shouldn’t be here. I should have let her go.

The problem is that every calculation she runs in her head leads to the same conclusion: Wanda tethered to Rio is Wanda in Agatha's sightline, and Wanda in Agatha's sightline means Agatha won't kill her yet. Because Agatha is theatrical. Agatha wants to drag this out, wants to make Rio suffer by degrees. But the moment Rio walks away, all bets are off. Wanda becomes a loose end instead of a game piece, and Agatha doesn't leave loose ends.

At least that's what Rio keeps telling herself when the guilt threatens to suffocate her. That this is strategy. That keeping Wanda close is the safest option. That it has nothing to do with the fact that Rio can’t bring herself to sever the last thread connecting her to the person she needs to be.

She brings everything out to the living room and joins Wanda on the couch. Their fingers brush softly together as she hands Wanda her wine glass, and she tries not to think about the way Agatha's presence lingers in this moment, suffuses it. How even this small gesture between them only exists because Agatha allowed it.

"Popcorn and wine," Wanda says with a teasing smile. “Dinner of champions."

"Well, it's no mushroom ragú with handmade pasta.” She means it as a joke, but her tone comes out wrong, somehow. Wanda gives a halfhearted smile, and then they fall into another silence, tender as a bruise.

“You sure you don’t want to talk about it?” Wanda ventures, but Rio just picks up the remote.

“Yup,” she says lightly. She flips on the TV and starts scrolling without waiting for a reply.

They both lean back into the couch as the movie begins, taking it in turns to sip their wine in silence. The bowl of popcorn rests between them.

Rio takes the opportunity to observe Wanda out of the corner of her eye. She’s staring at the screen, absentmindedly worrying her lower lip with her teeth; she doesn’t seem to be paying attention to the movie at all. She’s probably sitting there, thinking this is all her own fault, wondering if Rio is angry with her.

And Rio is angry. She's furious, suddenly, at herself, at Agatha. And yes, at Wanda – for staying, for putting up with it. For blaming herself, even after what she'd walked in on the other night. She'd seen the way Agatha had looked at Rio; she'd seen the way Rio had looked back at her. How could she bear to sit beside Rio like nothing is wrong, after seeing that? Why the fuck is she still here?

It’s not stupidity. Wanda isn't stupid. She's sitting here because she made a choice, because she looked at the evidence and decided Rio was worth believing in, and that – the trust, the sheer unearned generosity of it – makes something ugly rear up inside Rio's chest. It’s like being forgiven for something she can’t bear confessing to. Like being turned into a version of herself she no longer deserves to inhabit.

She’s here because she's good.

"Remember our first date?" Wanda asks out of the blue. Her eyes are still fixed to the screen.

“Yeah. Of course.”

“Remember how awkward we both were at first?”

Rio huffs a laugh. "Yeah."

Wanda turns to her, finally. “Are we okay?” she asks, and the look on her face is so worried and so sad that Rio moves the popcorn bowl from between them, setting it down on the coffee table. She shifts over, pulling Wanda in until her head is resting on Rio’s shoulder.

“We’re fine,” she says, laying her cheek on the top of Wanda’s head. “We’re going to be fine.”

Somehow she doesn't choke on the lie.

 

x

 

Rio wakes. Her brain is sluggish, sleepy; she's dimly aware that it's the middle of the night. She heard something, a sound, or – no. She felt something. The sensation of skin gliding against her own, warm and soft and slow. Fingertips glancing over her collarbone, across her shoulder, tracing the muscle of her bicep. She opens her eyes, and Agatha's face fills her vision, gorgeous and ethereal and dreamlike in the darkness.

"Shh," Agatha whispers as she leans in, enveloping Rio, tongue curling into her mouth. Heat rockets through Rio’s body. It should feel threatening, Agatha appearing here, in her bed, in the middle of the night. But it doesn’t feel threatening. It feels like a dream. Everything narrows, becomes soft lips and the nip of teeth and a desperate hunger that passes between them like an electric current as Agatha's hand slips beneath the waistband of Rio's boxers.

It must be a dream – it feels too good not to be a dream. Rio arches her back, pressing up into Agatha's hand.

"Don't move," Agatha breathes into her ear. "Stay still for me, baby."

Oh my god, Rio nearly groans, but Agatha's mouth covers hers again as her fingers begin working. Rio grabs onto her, holding on, nails digging into Agatha's skin as she tries to stay still, tries to obey, but her body takes over. She rides Agatha's hand, helpless. The scent of forest and tobacco and earth swirls around her, familiar and intoxicating and wild.

Agatha pulls out of the kiss and looks down at Rio, taking her in – her feverish desire, her need. She bites her lip as her eyes travel over Rio's face, like she could devour her right here. And then her gaze drops, followed quickly by the rest of her. Rio arches again as Agatha's lips close around her nipple. She's everywhere: lapping at Rio, sucking, fingers thrusting deep inside her.

Rio grasps a fistful of Agatha’s hair, pulling, like a warning, and then Agatha is there again, hovering over her.

“Aw, is it too much?” she whispers, grinning as her fingers slide over her, into her, again and again. “You can be quiet for me, can't you, baby? You can do this for me. Just take what I give you."

Rio whimpers softly. She's close, she's so close – she feels the pull of the river, the current carrying her under, the delicious drowning that only Agatha can provide, and she trembles as she gazes up into Agatha's eyes, trying to stay quiet as Agatha presses inside her again and again, slow and deep and so incredible—

Agatha wraps a hand around Rio's throat, fingers curling possessively, digging in hard enough to bruise, and that is what pushes Rio over the edge. She tries to scream as the orgasm rips through her, but she has no voice, no air. Agatha has all her air. She holds her there, pinning her down and working her through it until Rio sees stars.

Eventually Agatha releases her throat, finger by finger, and Rio takes a deep breath in. Her skin is aflame, heart thundering in her chest. Everything feels hazy and unreal, the room spinning around her as Agatha pushes herself to her feet. She smirks down at Rio, the glint in her eye victorious. Then she walks out of the room without another word.

It takes a few moments for Rio's heartbeat to slow, for the sound of blood to stop ringing in her ears. Silence descends around her, until all she can hear is the sound of breathing, soft and quiet. Her own.

And Wanda's.

She glances over at Wanda's sleeping form, the soft, peaceful look on her face, and she has to look away. She stares up at the ceiling as the horror of it descends all at once, sickening and awful.

It wasn't a dream – of course it wasn't a dream. She'd known it wasn't, even through the bleary fog of waking. She'd known. She just hadn't wanted to stop. It felt too good, too desperate and exhilarating.

She curls up into the fetal position facing away from Wanda and wraps her arms around herself. The smell of Agatha's perfume lingers on her skin as she takes long, deep breaths in. She's not going to cry; she doesn't deserve to cry about this. She grits her teeth and squeezes her eyes shut.

Who does something like this? Who the fuck does something like this, with their girlfriend right there? What the fuck is wrong with you?

When she opens her eyes again, they land on the portrait that Agatha had given her, still propped up on her dresser. She hadn't put it away, hadn't hidden it – stupidly, she hadn't wanted to move it. Because that’s where Agatha had decided it should go.

She stares into the eyes of the woman Agatha sees when she looks at her. It had seemed like a compliment before, to be painted this way. The woman in this picture could rule kingdoms, raze cities. She could ravage, destroy. She could do anything.

But maybe Rio doesn't want to be a woman who could do anything.

The portrait is shadowy in the dim light. She's looked at it every day since Agatha left it here – looked at it and looked away, again and again – but she stares at it now. The woman in the painting stares back.

The shades of blue look almost black in the dark. She’d noticed the color before, of course. It’s the first thing she’d noticed, the way Agatha had drenched her in it, built her out of it. Rio had taken it as atmospheric. She hadn't thought about what blue meant to Agatha. What Agatha does with blue.

Prussian blue. The paint she uses to create her cyanide.

Rio lies very still as the weight of it settles over her.

She painted me in poison.

The image stares at her like an accusation –You are made of this. You are mine – and Rio can’t believe she hadn’t seen it before. Hadn’t understood that this is the gift that Agatha brings. The only gift on offer: a web, a snare, a death trap. Everything she gives is barbed, tainted, and Rio will never be strong enough to escape it. She’s never going to be strong enough to resist. Anytime Agatha is near her she will fill Rio’s nose, her mouth, her lungs, and Rio will never be free. She will never be able to take another breath as long as she is Agatha’s.

This is who I am, she thinks, gazing at the painting. This is who she wants me to be.

A woman who could do anything.

She sits up in bed, and forces herself to look down at Wanda, curled up and sleeping beside her.

Alright, then. If that’s what she wants, so be it.

 

x

 

She takes the stairs slowly, unsure of what she might find at the bottom. Time feels strange, liquid, pooling around her like mercury. It gives everything an odd, distant quality. Her heart is only now starting to slow in her chest, her feet cold on the bare floors. Light streams out of the kitchen, throwing everything else into deep shadow, and Rio moves toward it, drawn in. Not so much walking as pulled.

Maybe she’s not here. Maybe I can walk back upstairs and convince myself it was a dream.

Maybe it was a dream.

But Agatha is here, of course. She’s standing at Rio’s sink, filling a glass of water, and she glances over her shoulder at the sound of Rio's footsteps. Her mouth curves into a slow smile.

"There she is."

Rio stops in the doorway. Every muscle in her body is trembling, and she clenches her fists to steady herself. She stares at Agatha – at the casual set of her shoulders, the unhurried way she turns around to lean back against the sink, the complete and total absence of anything resembling guilt or fear – and she tries to marshal the composure she’d found upstairs. So be it.

"Why did you do that?"

Agatha tilts her head. “You’ll have to be more specific."

"You know what I—” Rio stops. She’s not going to be goaded. “She was right there.”

Agatha just looks at her with those fathomless blue eyes, patient as stone.

“Did you come here to kill her?”

“If I’d come here to kill her, she’d be dead,” Agatha says simply.

“Did you come here to kill me?”

Agatha tilts her head slowly, and then she winks. She fucking winks.

Something cold moves through Rio.

"Here's what's going to happen," she says. "You're going to leave. Right now. Tonight. And you're never coming back."

Agatha's expression doesn't change. She doesn't argue, doesn't sneer, doesn't react at all – she just watches Rio with that infuriating patience, like she's waiting for her to finish. Like she has all the time in the world. Like Rio's demand is a child's tantrum she's decided to indulge.

The silence stretches. Rio waits for Agatha to say something, to push back, to give her something to fight against, but she doesn't.

"Did you hear me?" Rio asks.

"Mm." Agatha sets her glass down in the sink. "I heard you."

"Then—"

"Do you want me to kill her?" Agatha asks. “We could do it together, if you like. We could go back upstairs right now and—”

“What? No, I don’t want you to kill—”

“It would be easy. She looked so peaceful, sleeping next to—”

“Stop.” Rio’s voice shakes a little. “Just – go. Get out.”

Agatha studies her for a long moment. And then she sighs. “You know, I think I’ve been very patient with you, Rio,” she says. "I’ve let you and your little friends chase me all over the city. I’ve sat here quietly while you’ve played house with another woman. Kept my mouth shut when you fucked her in front of me – and frankly, the fact that she’s even alive after that is a testament to my heroic self-restraint. But my patience,” she says, voice going hard, “is not endless.”

Rio shakes her head slowly. “It doesn’t matter,” she says. “It’s over. I'm done with this."

“Yes. So you keep saying.”

“Because I mean it.”

"No you don't," Agatha says dismissively. “You’ve never meant it."

She takes a step toward Rio, and Rio steps back automatically, without thinking. That doesn’t stop Agatha. She just lifts an eyebrow and takes another step, and another, until there’s less than an arm’s length between them.

God, she’s beautiful. Rio hates herself for thinking it, but it’s what she’s always, always thinking. Agatha’s hair is pulled back into a messy braid, loose strands framing her face – her imperious cheekbones, dark eyebrows, lips pulled into a disdainful smile that Rio wants to trace with her fingertips, with her mouth.

And she knows what’s going to happen next: Agatha will take another step forward, and she’ll pull Rio in. Kiss her breathless, drag her down to the floor, and Rio will give in. She always gives in.

The woman in the painting never gives in.

Agatha starts to take a step forward, but she stops at the sight of the knife.

Rio had grabbed it before coming downstairs – pulled it from her bedside table with shaking hands, tucked it into the waistband of her boxers, felt the blade cold against her spine. She'd told herself it was just to be safe. Just to have something. She hadn't let herself think beyond that.

Agatha gazes at it for a moment. And then her face does something Rio hadn't anticipated: it relaxes, just slightly, into something that looks almost like pleasure.

“Well,” she says. “Look who finally found her fire.”

Rio is the one who steps forward now. She backs Agatha against the countertop, bringing the blade to her throat. Agatha doesn't fight, doesn't flinch. She tips her chin up slightly, and she smiles.

"Careful, baby,” she says. "You might hurt me."

"Shut up." The blade trembles in Rio’s grasp. "You're never going to stop, are you? You’re never going to leave me alone.”

"You don't want me to leave you alone.”

"I want you gone." It comes out low, and more desperate than she intends. "I want my life back. I want to stop having to think about you every time I—” She stops, clenching her teeth so hard her jaw aches. “I am done, Agatha.”

Something shifts in Agatha's expression. The amusement doesn't disappear, but something moves beneath it, dark shapes gliding under a placid sea.

“Hate to break it to you, sweetheart, but you don’t get to be done,” she says. Rio opens her mouth to protest, but Agatha cuts her off before she can speak. “Oh, I know. You still think you’re better than all othis. You think that I’m the bad one and you’re the good—”

“You are the bad one.”

“Am I?” Agatha’s lips curl into a smile. “Should we go upstairs and ask Blondie how good you are? I’ve watched you use that girl since the day you met her. You keep her around like she can give you somewhere to hide, but – do you know why you lie to her every chance you get? Do you know why you didn’t stop me when I crawled into your bed and fucked you in front of her?” Her smile is a sneer now, cruel and gloating. “Because you like it, Rio. You like hurting her. You get off on it.”

“I mean, I kill people, sure. But at least I don’t torture them first.”

"Get out," Rio growls. "Get out of my house."

Instead of leaving, Agatha closes her hand over Rio’s. Over the knife.

She guides Rio slowly, until the point of the blade is pressing into the curve of her own clavicle. Rio watches, unable to look away, as a slow line of red trickles down her chest, disappearing into her cleavage. I will never be strong enough to stop wanting this.

As long as she's here, I will never stop wanting her.

“Ooh,” Agatha says, teasing, mocking. “You want to play rough?”

"Yes."

This is who you made me.

“Alright,” Agatha says with a derisive snort. “Let’s see it, then. Let’s see you be brave for the first time in your sad little life.”

Don't you recognize me, Agatha? I'm a woman who can do anything.

"I can," Rio says.

And she does.

There’s resistance at first, the blade catching on skin and muscle – and then it travels swift and slick up to the hilt. Agatha’s mouth opens. Her eyes are wide with shock as they snap up to Rio’s.

Rio pulls.

The thud of the man’s head hitting the pavement—

Blood pooling on the driveway—

But Rio doesn’t need empty threats to push her into action. Her body just moves, and she lets go, follows her legs where they take her. Somewhere between the kitchen and the staircase she does the thing she’s best at: she becomes someone else entirely.

“She’s—”

Agatha is gone.

Chapter 12

Rio walks down the street, weaving past tourists, taking long strides. She likes the feel of the stretch in her legs, the crisp air in her lungs. Fall had finally given way to winter over the last few weeks, and she stuffs her hands into the pockets of her long coat, inhaling deeply.

For the first time in a long time, she doesn’t feel the need to look over her shoulder.

Today had been a good day. Lilia had asked her to meet with a rookie working on her first drug trafficking case, and they’d spent most of the day going over the file, pulling it apart, identifying gaps in the evidence and brainstorming ways to bolster what was there. It felt good to solve problems, to be trusted. It was like she had her feet under her again. The boxes of evidence in Agatha's case sat in the corner of her office, piled up next to the large snake plant. She'd walked past them a dozen times today without a second glance. That felt like something. Like… proof of something.

She has no idea if Agatha is alive or not. She’d spent the first few days after she’d stabbed her watching the news obsessively, scanning police reports, checking hospitals. Terrified and adrenalized, waiting for something. Confirmation, retribution. Something. But there’d been nothing.

The smell of blood stayed with her wherever she went for those first few days. And the nightmares – she kept waking with her hand clenched around the slick-slip of a knife, Agatha’s pained face floating in front of her eyes. But no matter how many times she dreamed about her, how many times her unconscious mind sought her out, there was no sign of her.

Rio wasn’t sure what to think. Agatha had felt for so long like an inevitability: she was everywhere, she could get to Rio anytime she wanted. She knew everything, saw everything – she was ghost, wraith, witch. She must be dead, Rio kept thinking. She must be. She wouldn’t just let me go.

But maybe she had. Maybe Agatha wasn’t dead; maybe Rio had finally crossed a line that even Agatha wouldn’t dare uncross. Maybe the answer to How do I get out of this? had always been At the point of a knife.

She’s not sure whether to declare victory. None of this is how she imagined it would be.

This is freedom, she says to herself, over and over again. This is what freedom feels like.

Tonight, though, she doesn’t need convincing. Tonight she is walking down the street, soft yellow lights pouring out of the buildings she passes, collar up, warm against the chill. She’s kicking ass at work. She’s going to meet her girlfriend for dinner. They’re trying a new restaurant. All of it is effortless, easy. She’d almost forgotten what it felt like.

I want you to feel this, Agatha’s voice whispers in her ear.

Rio doesn’t stop walking.

"Oh my god – it's getting cold out there!" Rio says, shrugging off her coat. She sits down, glancing around the restaurant. “Oh, wow. This place is great."

There are plants built into the walls, long vines trailing down next to waterfalls. It's dark and cozy, low light filtering through the leaves; the effect is something like having dinner in the middle of a forest. Rio leans back in her chair and grins.

"Okay, I have to tell you about this rookie I met today. She was an art history major and I was telling her all about you and – long story short, you might be getting a call from a tiny baby agent who's desperate to talk art with someone who actually knows something about it. I hope that's okay. She just moved here, I don't think she has any friends yet.” She regards the glass in Wanda’s hand. “Anyway. What are you drinking?"

Wanda doesn't reply. She takes a sip of her wine and places her glass on the table in silence.

Rio gives her a beat, and then another, until it becomes clear Wanda isn’t planning on saying anything at all.

“Uh… is everything okay?" she asks. A stupid question; it very plainly isn’t. “What’s wrong?”

Instead of answering, Wanda reaches into her purse and pulls out her phone. She unlocks it and sets it down on the table between them.

“Press play,” she says.

“…What is it?” Rio asks.

Wanda just stares at her.

“Yeah. Uh. Okay.”

Rio leans forward. The phone is open to one of Wanda's voicemails. She presses play.

“Fuck – oh, FUCK – oh my god—”

The pornographic sounds blast through the quiet restaurant at full volume.

“Jesus – Christ, Wanda,” Rio hisses. Every head in the place whips around as she fumbles with the phone, picking it up, dropping it, trying to turn it off. “What the fuck?”

“Oh god – don’t stop, don’t stop, don—”

Rio finally manages to mute the phone. Her cheeks burn; she can feel a hundred pairs of eyes on her in the sudden deafening silence. An aggrieved-looking waiter is making his way toward their table, but he pauses, shooting her a stern look once it’s clear the noise has stopped.

“You pocket-dialed me last night,” Wanda says in an even voice. “It went to voicemail. I was at book club.”

No, Rio thinks, heart pounding in her chest, her stomach, her fingertips. She’s gone. I protected us from her. I thought I had protected us from her.

A childish fantasy. Of course Agatha had recorded them when they were together. Of course she had. Rio had been so transfixed by the knife in her own hand that she hadn’t bothered checking for the weapons Agatha was carrying.

She swallows, tongue like sandpaper. “Wanda—”

“Don’t,” Wanda snaps in a low voice. “Do not.”

Rio briefly closes her mouth, but she can’t stop herself. It’s a compulsion, a need. The words are structural at this point – load-bearing half-truths and outright deceptions. Even now that the ground is shaking beneath her feet, her first instinct is to grab for the walls and desperately try to shore them up.

“That wasn’t me,” she says, the words tumbling out.

As lies go, they both recognize that one is pathetic.

“She says your fucking name, Rio,” Wanda spits. “Do you want to hear more?”

She reaches for the phone again, and—

“No,” Rio says quickly, snaking out a hand to intercept her arm. “No.”

Wanda glares down at where Rio’s fingers are wrapped around her wrist, and Rio lets her go.

“How long?” Wanda asks, icy eyes lifting to meet Rio’s.

It’s not like Rio hadn’t thought about what to do if Wanda found out. The excuses she might make. But she hadn’t been expecting this – she hadn’t been expecting proof. A recording, witnesses all around them, having to look Wanda in the eye knowing she'd heard the words she and Agatha had said to one another—

“Obviously you’ve been fucking her since before the dinner,” Wanda says. “You remember that, right? When you made me think I was crazy for suspecting something was going on between you two?”

The venom in her tone doesn’t hide the way her voice breaks a little on the words, and tears prick at Rio’s eyes. God. A hole could open beneath her right now and she'd climb into it gratefully. She never, ever should have gone anywhere near this woman.

“I bet you really enjoyed that. Probably had a good laugh about it together later, didn’t you? Stupid, pathetic Wanda, can’t even see what’s right in front of her,” Wanda says, shaking her head and wiping at the tears on her cheeks. “God. I am such an idiot.”

“No, you’re not—”

“Don’t,” Wanda snaps. “Do you think I want you to comfort me right now?”

Rio drops her eyes again. “Sorry,” she mumbles.

Wanda’s laugh is incredulous and a little manic, something on the edge of hysteria. “Sorry? Oh, you’re sorry. Okay. Well, then. She’s sorry, everyone,” she says, raising her voice.

Eyes flicker over to them again, the entire restaurant furtively watching the show. “Could we just—” Rio says through gritted teeth. “Can we please do this somewhere else?”

“Oh no, am I embarrassing you, Rio? Are you feeling completely and utterly humiliated?”

Wanda’s face is a mask of grief and rage, lips trembling, but after a moment she closes her eyes and takes a moment to compose herself. She inhales, one breath in, then out. And then she leans back in her chair and picks up her wine glass again, her movements practiced, almost casual.

Rio braces.

“So, funny story. I looked Agnes up. She’s not answering her phone, so I was trying to track her down to let her know I think she’s a huge fucking bitch. And you know what I found?”

Rio can feel the walls buckling, crumbling beneath her hands. “What?”

“Nothing,” Wanda replies. “She doesn’t have any social media. I mean, high-powered exec like that, and she doesn’t even show up on LinkedIn? That’s weird. So then I paid for one of those background check things online, and guess what? There is no Agnes O’Connor that matches her description. No one the right age or location.” She takes a sip of her wine, then gestures toward Rio with the glass. “You fucked a ghost.”

Rio just sits there, trembling. Waiting.

“Nothing to say to that?” Wanda asks lightly. “Okay, well, whatever. Jen will figure it out.”

Rio’s stomach drops through the floor.

“You talked to Jen,” she says, dry-mouthed.

Wanda nods. “Yeah. She was pretty pissed. Asked me three times if I was sure about the name. Think she’s planning on paying your girlfriend a visit.” She grimaces theatrically. “Your professional reputation might be taking a bit of a hit, so, sorry about that.” She twirls the glass in her hand. “But hey, I apologized. That makes it okay, right?”

Rio’s heart is in her throat, the pressure so intense that for a few panicked moments she can’t hear or think or breathe. She closes her eyes.

That’s it, then.

That’s her career, gone. Her freedom. That’s all of it, her entire life, giving way and crashing down around her.

The look on Lilia’s face when she finds out – her siblings – god, her parents—

It doesn’t matter. It’s too late now.

It’s over.

For half a second the thought almost feels like relief, but it’s a tainted, sickening kind of relief, the relief of a swarm of flies rising off a carcass. She looks out over one ruinous choice after the other, all of them unfurling behind her, and after all of it she can’t even explain why. She was bored, yes, and Agatha was intriguing and sexy and dangerous, but none of that was the reason. Lots of people get bored. They go on a trip or something. They get a motorcycle. Why didn’t she just buy a fucking motorcycle?

Because you like it, Rio.

She opens her eyes again to find Wanda staring at her. And in the split second before Wanda has the chance to hide, Rio catches it. The thing underneath the anger. All of Wanda’s grief, her exhaustion. The face of a person whose walls are collapsing, too.

“Wanda,” Rio says hoarsely. “I really am sorry.”

The mask of rage flickers back into place in the span of a heartbeat.

“I cannot explain to you how much I do not care that you are sorry,” Wanda spits. “I want to know how long.”

Rio looks at her. Before, during. The whole time.

"Not long," she says.

Wanda just stares at her for a moment. Then she laughs, short and acrid, like Rio’s just confirmed something she already knew. “Right,” she says. “I don’t know why I bothered asking a liar for the truth.”

“Wanda—”

“No,” Wanda says, standing up from her chair. She picks up her coat and tugs it on, plunging her arms into the sleeves. “No, you know what, Rio? You just keep sitting there, spouting your bullshit. I don’t actually need an apology from you. I just want you to leave me alone. I want you to never contact me again. And I want you to know—” She finishes buttoning her coat and looks over at Rio, licking salty tears off her lips. “I want you to know that after I walk out this door, I will never think of you again.”

Wanda brushes past her and walks away. She leaves Rio at the table, shadowed beneath the canopy of leaves, gazing down at her empty hands.

 

x

 

She isn’t sure how she gets home. She doesn’t remember the walk from the restaurant to the parking garage, doesn’t remember climbing into her car, or driving – but she must have, because when she finally registers that she’s in her driveway she’s still sitting in the driver’s seat. She shuts off the engine and stares straight ahead, blinking.

That was bad, is about all the thought she can muster. That was really bad.

She squeezes her eyes shut. She’s not going to cry; she doesn’t deserve to cry about this. None of it should be a surprise. Rio’s been lighting match after match for months, telling herself the heat would keep her warm. Eventually some spark was bound to catch. What did she think was going to happen?

It’s only when she opens her eyes again that she finally notices the woman sitting on the steps of her front porch.

Her first instinct is to turn the car back on and throw it into reverse, peel out, drive off and never come back. But the impulse doesn’t make it to her muscles, not even enough to make them twitch. Running is pointless. It probably always has been. There’s no escaping what comes next.

Rio pushes the car door open and climbs out. She approaches slowly, pulling her coat more tightly around her. The cold air edges down her collar anyway, seeps up her sleeves.

It’s going to freeze soon, she thinks, envisioning the feel of her fingers plunging into the dirt, pulling up dead flowers by the roots.

She stops at the bottom of the porch and opens her mouth to speak, but Lilia holds up a hand before she can.

“Don’t. You don’t say a word to me or to anyone without your union rep present. Got it?”

Lilia is gazing up at her from where she’s sitting on the stairs. Her face isn’t giving anything away, but it doesn’t have to. Rio can read her disappointment in her posture, in the way she’s holding herself, too rigid, like she’s trying to protect herself from this.

There are so many things Rio could say, and every one of them would be the truth or a lie. Either way, it would just make things worse. And so Rio nods, finally, for lack of anything else to do.

“I don’t know what you got yourself mixed up in, kid,” Lilia says after a long moment, climbing to her feet so that Rio has to tilt her chin to look up at her, a child waiting for punishment. “Do you have your badge on you?”

No, Rio almost lies, like a reflex. Like it would make any difference.

Match after match after match.

She pulls the badge off her belt and hands it to Lilia, who frowns down at it. Lilia looks grim, nearly pained as she examines the shield, this proof of Rio’s fidelity to the Agency, proof of their faith in her. But when her eyes lift again to meet Rio’s, she only looks resigned.

“Your service weapon?”

Rio swallows. “In my car.”

“Go get it.”

Rio nods again. She turns around, and begins retracing her steps through the cold.

You need to breathe, and you need to think, she tells herself. Agatha wouldn’t take this lying down; it would just be part of the game. God – this would probably be fun for her.

Think.

Rio’s been an agent for a long time; she knows how to construct a narrative. All she has to do is build a story credible enough to save herself. Her career, too, if she can manage it, but right now she needs to focus on staying out of prison. Reasonable doubt. That’s all she needs. And what do they really know?

Wanda told Jen that Rio had cheated on her with a woman named Agnes O’Connor, and Jen obviously clocked the name immediately. She’d run to Lilia with her suspicions that Rio had been quite literally sleeping with the enemy, and that left Lilia with no choice but to suspend her while they investigate.

But what do they have for proof?

Rio could argue that she’s been set up. That this was Agatha’s plan from the beginning – to infiltrate the investigation, turn them against one another, create chaos. It’s what she’s done from day one.

And yeah, okay, Wanda will be able to provide first-hand testimony that Rio had been in the same place as Agatha several times, and had done nothing about it. Called her by a fake name. Nearly kissed her in Wanda’s kitchen. And the phone message, the sex. None of that looks great.

But she could tell them that Agatha had threatened to kill Wanda – that’s not even a lie. She could say that she’d been trying to figure out how to make the arrest without Wanda getting caught in the crossfire. And the voicemail… she’ll come up with something. She'll be able to prove her number was spoofed, so who's to say that any of it was real? AI can do anything these days, right? She just needs reasonable doubt. If she can paint all of this as some fucked up plan of Agatha’s to ruin Rio’s life – also not a lie, really – then it might be enough to get her out of this. She’ll be in trouble for failing to make the arrest, yes. She’ll probably get fired. Might face some jail time, but a good lawyer can get her a decent plea. She can get through this. Her life won’t be over.

The lockbox under her passenger seat beeps with the correct code, and Rio reaches in and retrieves her gun. The metal is cool against her palm, satisfyingly heavy. She’s never been one of those agents who was comforted by the idea that she had the option to use deadly force; her weapon was a last resort, and she treated it like one. But only now that she’s being asked to hand it over does she realize she’s gotten used to having it here. She’s spent the last fifteen years knowing, in the back of her mind, that she always had a way to protect herself. Being asked to give it up feels like being asked to dismantle her fortress walls, kick open the front gate. It’s leaving her too exposed, too vulnerable.

You didn’t need a gun with Agatha, though, did you?

Stop. Not now. Not yet.

Think.

By the time she places the weapon in Lilia’s outstretched hand, her heartbeat is steady again. She has a plan. She’s in a bad situation with a narrow path out, yes. But she’s not standing on a cliff at the edge of the world.

Still, Lilia’s words, when they come, feel an awful lot like falling.

“Agent Vidal, I am placing you on administrative leave pending investigation. You’ve been instructed to surrender your credentials and your service weapon. I am ordering you not to have contact with any individual connected to the investigation, and you will have no access to FBI facilities or systems during your suspension from duty. You are entitled to union representation throughout this process, including now. Would you like to contact your union representative?”

Rio can’t help herself. “Can I just—”

“Union rep,” Lilia snaps, cutting her off. “Yes or no?”

The cold stings Rio’s eyes. “No.”

“The Office of Professional Responsibility will conduct the investigation. Any failure to cooperate will result in your immediate termination. Do you understand the terms of your suspension as I’ve explained them here?”

Rio nods, biting the inside of her cheek to keep the tears from falling.

For a long moment Lilia just looks at her. Something moves across her face – something so similar to what Rio had seen on Wanda’s face, that same fatigued sadness – and then she exhales, long and slow, like she's been holding it since she sat down on Rio’s porch.

“Yeah. Bad day, kiddo," she says, shaking her head slowly. "Really bad day."

She doesn't wait for a response. Rio listens to the sound of her footsteps on the walkway until she can't hear them anymore.

 

x

 

She collapses onto her couch after Lilia leaves. Her eyes won’t focus – she's too dazed to comprehend anything but the middle distance. She has no idea how much time passes as she sits there in her too-quiet house, unable to move or feel or decide what to do next.

Her thoughts flit past her, and she regards each of them as if from very far away: she should call someone. Her union rep. A lawyer. Her sister, maybe. She picks up her phone once, stares at it until the screen goes dark, and sets it down again. She can't seem to hold onto anything.

At some point she registers that she's still wearing her coat. All the cold has bled out of it now. She doesn't take it off.

It occurs to her, distantly, that she hasn't eaten since lunch. That she can't remember what she had for lunch. That she didn’t eat dinner—

The thought darts mercifully away before she can finish following it.

She's interviewed enough people in shock to recognize it. The brain protecting itself – but it’ll pass. It always passes. Eventually she’ll have to deal with what comes next. She’ll have to make the calls. She’ll have to sit across from an OPR investigator and decide, sentence by sentence, how much of her life she's willing to give up to save the rest of it.

But not yet.

The tears finally arrive the way water seeps through cracks in the earth – not the relief of weeping, but something closer to a trickle. She doesn't wipe them away.

She isn't thinking. She thinks about the not-thinking. About what isn’t here. The Agatha-shaped hollow in the room, the one she’s looked past for months, the one that her grief and her guilt and her ruined career are currently arranging themselves around, vines creeping toward light.

Agatha is still alive.

The thought simply surfaces, this thing she has been diligently not-thinking all evening.

She’s alive.

There's still a chance she isn't, of course. There’s a chance that the voicemail that went out to Wanda was some sort of dead man's switch, set to send if Rio ever became bold enough to strike back. But Rio doubts it. Agatha hadn't believed Rio would hurt her – neither of them had, not really, not until the knife slid its way past skin and muscle and nestled itself deep inside her viscera. Then they knew, both of them.

Like Rio knows that Agatha is alive: with a certainty in the shape of a wound.

What she doesn't know is what it means. Whether she needs to hide or fight; whether she’ll have a choice, in the end. But she can feel her out there – ghost, wraith, witch, the enemy at the gate. Inevitable as she's always been. Still toying with her. Still playing the game.

So what are you going to do about it?

She's always been fascinated by people like Agatha. Always been drawn to darkness. Being in her presence is like a bell ringing, like coming home, like the pain of setting a bone.

But Agatha did this. Dismantled her life, ground her down to nothing. Left her with ash.

(A reckoning, or an offering?)

(Both. With Agatha it’s always both.)

Here is what Rio knows, a thought so bright and true that she can hold it in her hands: Agatha is still alive.

For now.

Chapter 13

Rio stares up at the ceiling.

Her eyes trace the familiar crack in the plaster over the couch. She’s looked at it a thousand times, but she still hasn’t gotten around to fixing it. Next weekend, she’d thought every time, and every time next weekend had come and gone.

She’d always loved this house. It needed a lot of work when she bought it – which was the only reason she’d been able to afford it – but she'd rolled up her sleeves and made it happen. Ripping out old carpeting, pulling at the tacks in the floors until her fingernails bled. Stripping the kitchen cabinets to reveal the wood underneath. Knocking through drywall with a sledgehammer, then rebuilding. She can remember standing in the middle of her living room and feeling like her heart lived in the walls of this place.

She inhales, cigarette flaring in the darkness.

She can't find her. After spending the last several months only pretending to look for Agatha, now that she really, truly needs to know where she is, needs it like an ache in her teeth – nothing. She’d had every resource in the world, the authority of the United States government on a badge at her hip, and what had she done with it? Spun her fucking wheels. For months. Playacted at being an agent. Last week it had felt like some sort of victory to walk past Agatha’s files without giving them a second glance. She’d be willing to gnaw off her own arm for those files right now.

She exhales smoke in a thin stream between her lips and lifts her phone again.

Woman stabbed DC

Agnes O’Connor

Agatha Harkness

There’s nothing new since she typed in the same searches ten minutes ago. Obviously.

Her phone screen shows 3:23am. She ought to go to bed, except she knows she’s just going to do the same thing there. Ruminate and Google. Might as well save herself the trip.

She lets her phone fall onto her chest and flicks ash onto a plate on the floor beside her. The mirror across the room reflects the movement, and she traces the outline of herself there: long legs poking out of boxers, the same oversized shirt she’s been wearing for days. She can’t see her face; the mirror’s not angled for it. Probably for the best.

She picks up her phone again, blinking into the cold light, and then she bares her teeth and growls at it.

“You’re fucking pathetic,” she announces to no one as she lets the phone fall to the floor.

She can find people. AFIS, NGI, CODIS – if she’d really been looking, she’d have found Agatha months ago. She was good. With the right tools, she was good. But they took everything away from her, and all she’s got now are the strategies of every other scorned woman with a phone. She might as well be some fucking civilian stalker. Without her badge, it turns out, she’s nothing but useless want.

She throws an arm over her eyes. She’s been trying for days to think her way out of it, to come up with a less humiliating plan, but at this point it’s all she’s got.

“Fine,” she tells the empty room. “Fine. Fuck.”

 

x

 

The suit-wearing morning crowd fills the coffee shop with a competent impatience, everyone bustling around, eager to get to their jobs, live their lives. A week ago, these were Rio’s people. Well – no. These were never her people. But she hadn’t felt like a stranger among them, at least.

She probably should have worn a suit too, tried to blend in, but when she stared into her closet this morning all of her sleek professional clothes looked as artificial as costumes. The trappings of some other person’s life. She couldn’t bear the thought of it, a crisp button-down against her skin, suit jacket tight on her shoulders, so instead she’d plunged a hand into her dresser drawers and pulled on the first things she grabbed – a ratty Fleetwood Mac concert tee that she’d thrifted years ago, black and wrinkled, and worn black jeans. She’d shrugged on her leather jacket and left the house before she could talk herself out of it.

She squints against the too-bright morning sun streaming through the windows as she shuffles forward, slowly making her way to the front of the line.

“She returns,” the barista says when it’s finally Rio’s turn. She’s already punching her order in. “Haven’t seen you around in a while. I started to think you were cheating on me with another coffee shop.”
